<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

		<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.impactStatement || vm.details.abstract || vm.details.scope || vm.details.purpose  || vm.details.previewImage --><div ng-if="::vm.details.impactStatement || vm.details.abstract || vm.details.scope || vm.details.purpose  || vm.details.previewImage" ng-class="vm.details.graphicalAbstract ? 			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract --><div ng-if="::vm.details.abstract" class="ng-scope">
				<strong>Abstract:</strong>
				<div class="abstract-text ng-binding" ng-bind-html="::vm.details.abstract">This letter proposes wearable electronic modules on textile, suitable for garment integration, based on antennas magnetically coupled to the active circuitry. The coupling mechanism is based on a heterogeneous transformer with the primary and secondary windings implemented in the antenna and in the circuitry substrate (hybrid or monolithic), respectively. The proposed coupling topology avoids galvanic contacts between the antenna and the active circuitry, allowing for interconnecting the antenna by a mere placing and gluing process. Bonding and soldering processes, often critical for textile materials, are thus avoided. The proposed innovation, mitigating the industrial realization constraints posed by textile implementation of active antennas and radio frequency identification (RFID) tags, should unsurprisingly enable and foster industrial developments of garment RFID, wearable electronics and body-centric communications systems. A patch antenna with transformer based on textile materials and operating in the frequency band 2.4-2.4835 GHz is designed, realized, and measured.</div>
			</div><!-- end ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract -->			
			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.scope -->

This technique relies on a copper adhesive tape that is shaped by a photo-lithographic process and then transferred to the hosting substrate by means of a sacrificial layer. Microstrip lines in paper substrates have been electromagnetically characterized accounting also for the adhesive layers. Experimental results show an insertion loss better than 0.6 dB/cm at 10 GHz.&quot;,&quot;pdfSize&quot;:&quot;352KB&quot;,&quot;openUrlImgLoc&quot;:&quot;http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/assets/img/btn.find-in-library.png&quot;},&quot;refType&quot;:&quot;biblio&quot;,&quot;id&quot;:&quot;ref6&quot;},{&quot;order&quot;:&quot;7&quot;,&quot;text&quot;:&quot;A. <div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>Automatic identification procedures and wearable textile systems are, nowadays, attractive fields of research. The radio frequency identification (RFID) systems play an important role in automatic identification and monitoring procedures. RFID allows objects and their neighboring environment to become self-describing by communicating and monitoring their identity and relevant information. Wearable RFIDs and smart fabrics and interactive textiles (SFITs) rely on the unobtrusive integration of electronic systems into garments <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a>, ultimately enabling wearable intelligence. Intelligent wearable systems extend the functionality of professional garments (for instance the protection level) by providing more information about the wearer's operating conditions, such as health state and environmental parameters, by means of wearable sensors networks potentially integrated in body area networks (BANs). Without lack of generality, in this letter we focus on firefighter and emergency disaster personnel garments <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a>.</p><p>SFIT systems as well as wearable RFIDs require that active electronic circuits (sensors and control units) transmit and receive wirelessly via antenna systems. Generally, these subsystems, antennas, and circuits are directly interconnected by means of galvanic contacts, thus requiring a wire-bonding or a soldering process. Recently, a novel method to connect such subsystems has been proposed in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_1">[6]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_1">[7]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_1">[8]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_1">[10]</a>. This method is based on magnetic coupling and promises to enhance the integration level of wearable electronic systems. In particular, for the simple yet nonrestrictive antenna-chip case, a transformer performs the coupling by means of two windings realized on the antenna and electronic substrates, respectively. As a result, the chip (or the hybrid circuit) can be mounted by a mere placing and gluing process <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_1">[11]</a>. In this way, the chip is padless and completely passivated; bonding and soldering processes, critical for textile materials, are avoided. It is worth noticing that this feeding topology works similarly to aperture coupling <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a> (the coupling is provided without direct contact), but in this case a lumped element is used. The advantage of this approach is the compactness of the transformer, fundamental for low-frequency application and an on-chip implementation <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_1">[8]</a>; the transformer dimensions are smaller than distributed elements like the aperture that is proportional to the wavelength.</p><p>The antenna-transformer prototype has been designed, fabricated, and measured. The transformer's primary winding has been integrated onto an antenna and implemented on the same textile substrate without loss of generality. The secondary winding has been realized on a flexible substrate for test purposes. The design of the antenna and the transformer aims at maximizing the power transfer between the antenna and the load (emulating the chip in the test application) connected to the secondary winding. The first step is the design and the optimization of the transformer. Next, a patch antenna is designed. The antenna is matched to the transformer optimum impedance, resulting in a transmission coefficient close to the maximum available gain (MAG). Finally, the prototype is measured, and the results are compared to the simulations.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Transformer and Antenna Design</h2></div><p>The antenna and the one-turn primary winding are realized on textile materials (Flectron and black foam) <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref12" id="context_ref_12_2">[12]</a>. The one-turn secondary winding is realized on a flexible substrate (DuPont Pyralux) <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref13" id="context_ref_13_2">[13]</a>, and its terminals are connected to a UFL connector, necessary to characterize the antenna-transformer system by means of the vector network analyzer (VNA). The properties of the materials used are listed in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a>. The conductivity of Flectron was estimated from the sheet resistance (nominal value <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1" role="math" style="display: inline-block; The material stack-up, starting from the bottom layer, is as follows:

<div class="figure figure-full table" id="table1"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Table I </b>Materials Properties<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/6719497/6716005/6716005-table-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/6719497/6716005/6716005-table-1-small.gif" alt="Table I" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div>
<p></p><div class="section_2" id="sec2a"><h3>A. Transformer Design</h3><p>The first step consists of designing the transformer. The transformer is inherently heterogeneous because the materials and the dimensions are, in general, different for the two windings. The two one-turn windings have an inner radius of 1 mm and a track width of 1 mm. The transformer dimensions result from a compromise between the performance (transmission coefficient), the reliability, and the technological constraints imposed by the fabrication process (design rules). The primary Flectron winding on the black foam is implemented in a square aperture (side 10 mm long) on the antenna ground plane. The secondary copper winding is realized on a small flexible Pyralux substrate with a standard photolithographic process, enabling a reliable interconnection with active electronic circuits and in the meantime maintaining flexibility and wearability of the module. A sheet of bi-adhesive tape separates both windings.</p><p>The transformer layout, shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a> and without antenna and UFL connector, was simulated with an electromagnetic (EM) simulator in order to find the optimum dimensions. The MAG of the heterogeneous transformer, in the case of simultaneous conjugate matching, is equal to â0.27 dB. At 2.45 GHz, the source (the antenna) and the load (the chip) optimum impedances of the transformer are found to be <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-14" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 10.874em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 9.793em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1009.73em 2.526em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-15" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-16" style="display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; <p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2b"><h3>B. Antenna Design</h3><p>Once the optimal termination impedances of the transformer are known, the following step consists of designing the antenna. The antenna operates in the Industrial, Scientific, and Medical (ISM) frequency band for short-range devices (SRDs) (2.4â2.4835 GHz). The patch antenna topology has been chosen because the antenna ground plane shields the body from antenna radiation and stabilizes the antenna characteristics in proximity of the human body, thereby mitigating the adverse effects that typically occur in body-centric communication applications <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_2b">[2]</a>.</p><p>
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig2"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/6719497/6716005/6716005-fig-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/6719497/6716005/6716005-fig-2-small.gif" alt="Figure 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 2. </b><fig><p>Optimized patch antenna: reflection coefficient <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-7-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-60" role="math" style="display: inline-block; Reflection is computed with respect to the complex conjugate source impedance of the transformer <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-8-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-69" role="math" style="display: inline-block; Computer-aided optimization by means of full-wave simulations has been performed to match the antenna impedance to the optimum source impedance of the transformer by varying the feeding point position, as in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_2b">[2]</a> and <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_2b">[3]</a>. This procedure yields the maximum power transfer over the transformer to be obtained throughout the desired bandwidth. The simulation results in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a> refer to a square patch with a length of 44 mm. The input impedance is equal to 93.23-j43.17 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-9-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-103" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 0.844em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.724em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.66em 2.345em -999.997em); The radiation efficiency is â1.74 dB (67.5%), and the maximum gain is 6.71 dBi.<p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2c"><h3>C. Sensitivity Analysis</h3><p>A simulation campaign of the structure antenna-transformer was performed in order to analyze the effects of the distance (tape thickness) and horizontal misalignment variations of the windings. <div class="figure figure-full" id="fig4"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/6719497/6716005/6716005-fig-4-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/6719497/6716005/6716005-fig-4-small.gif" alt="Figure 4" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 4. </b><fig><p>Radiation efficiency of the transformer-antenna system, varying the horizontal misalignment between the windings for a constant tape thickness equal to 100 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-15-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-165" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 0.797em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.599em; height: 0px; font-size: 126%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.591em 1000.6em 2.649em -999.997em); top: -2.245em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-166" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-167" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Math/Italic/336/03BC.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 9px; height: 11px; vertical-align: -4px; margin-right: 0.003em;" /></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.252em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.329em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.004em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-15">\mu</script></tex-math></inline-formula>m.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6716005/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
The radiation efficiency <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-16-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-168" role="math" style="display: inline-block; top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-169" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-170" style="display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.024em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.426em 1000.48em 4.387em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-171" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Math/Italic/336/03B7.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 8px; height: 11px; vertical-align: -4px; margin-right: 0.003em;" /><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.483em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-172" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-173" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-174" style="display: inline-block; font-size: 70.7%;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Math/Italic/238/005A.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 8px; height: 8px; margin-right: -0.057em;" /><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.263em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.803em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-16">\eta_{Z}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> (with respect to the project optimum impedance equal to 80-j103.2 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-17-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-175" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 0.844em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.724em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.66em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-176" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-177" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/03A9.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 11px; height: 11px; margin-right: 0.063em;" /></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-17">\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> at 2.45 GHz) as a function of the distance and the misalignment is plotted in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Figs. 3</a> and <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">4</a>, respectively. Its behavior is affected by the reflection coefficient, considering that <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-18-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-178" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 0.544em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.483em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.48em 2.526em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-179" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-180" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Math/Italic/336/03B7.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 8px; height: 11px; vertical-align: -4px; margin-right: 0.003em;" /><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.263em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.803em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-18">\eta</script></tex-math></inline-formula> does not change significantly when distance and misalignment vary. The efficiency <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-19-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-181" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 1.144em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.024em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1001.02em 2.526em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-182" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-183" style="display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.024em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.426em 1000.48em 4.387em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-184" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Math/Italic/336/03B7.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 8px; height: 11px; vertical-align: -4px; margin-right: 0.003em;" /><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.483em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-185" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-186" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-187" style="display: inline-block; font-size: 70.7%;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Math/Italic/238/005A.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 8px; height: 8px; margin-right: -0.057em;" /><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.263em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.803em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-19">\eta_{Z}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> decreases as a function of the misalignment, but it remains quite constant for an offset up to 2 mm. Yet, even for a large misalignment of 4 mm, we still obtain an efficiency of 36.5%. The windings distance depends on the bi-adhesive tape thickness, and this parameter can be controlled accurately. Meanwhile, the efficiency results to be robust with respect to the misalignment that depends on the alignment tolerance of the mounting process.<p></p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Results</h2></div><p>Finally, the overall system antenna-transformer was fabricated and measured. The prototype before and after the assembly is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 5</a>. One contact of the one-turn primary winding realized on the patch ground plane is connected to the patch by means of a via, whereas the other contact is connected to the ground plane.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig5"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/6719497/6716005/6716005-fig-5-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/7727/6719497/6716005/6716005-fig-5-small.gif" alt="Figure 5" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 5. </b><fig><p>Prototypes of the antenna-transformer: (a) front view and (b) back view of the antenna with the primary winding; (c) top view and (d) bottom view of the Pyralux square with the secondary winding and the UFL connector; (e) assembled prototype with bi-adhesive tape. After assembly with bi-adhesive tape, the windings are not visible anymore (the primary is covered and the secondary is on the other side of the Pyralux square), and only the UFL connector is accessible (zoomed area).</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6716005/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>

The complex conjugate of the load optimum impedance and of the gain circles (step of â0.25 dB) are obtained at 2.45 GHz.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/6716005/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>The radiation efficiency and the maximum gain, obtained from the simulation of the entire system, are equal to â1.96 dB (63.6%) and 6.49 dBi, respectively, at 2.45 GHz. The maximum gain is similar to the results obtained by measuring other square patch antennas <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_3">[2]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_3">[3]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_3">[4]</a>. This means that the feeding topology does not significantly affect the antenna performance. Studies about the bending effects on patch antennas are shown in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_3">[2]</a>â<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_3">[3]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_3">[4]</a>.</p><p>

The results are shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig6" class="fulltext-link">Figs. 6</a> and <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">7</a>. The simulated and measured reflection coefficients are plotted and compared on the Smith chart with reference impedance of 50 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-26-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-241" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 0.844em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.724em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.66em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-242" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-243" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Main/Regular/336/03A9.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 11px; height: 11px; margin-right: 0.063em;" /></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-26">\Omega</script></tex-math></inline-formula> in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig6" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 6</a> : The simulated results are obtained by simulating the overall antenna-transformer structure (dashed line) and by cascading the two-port <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-27-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-244" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 0.664em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.604em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.6em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-245" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-246" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Math/Italic/336/0053.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 10px; height: 12px; vertical-align: -1px; margin-right: -0.057em;" /><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-27">S</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-parameters of the transformer and the one-port <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-28-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-247" role="math" style="display: inline-block; width: 0.664em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.604em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.6em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-248" style="display: inline-block;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-249" style="display: inline-block;"><img src="http://staticieeexplore.ieee.org/xploreAssets/MathJax-2.6.1/fonts/HTML-CSS/TeX/png/Math/Italic/336/0053.png?rev=2.6.1" style="width: 10px; height: 12px; vertical-align: -1px; margin-right: -0.057em;" /><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.063em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-28">S</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-parameters of the antenna (black dotted line) simulated separately during their design. The magnitude of the measured reflection coefficient is plotted in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 7</a> to verify the impedance bandwidth in the operating band.<p></p><p>A comparison of the present results to the state of Art (SoA) of textile patch antennas operating at 2.45 GHz is summarized in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table2" class="fulltext-link">Table II</a>.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Conclusion</h2></div><p>A textile antenna with a transformer-based feeding topology, integrable into garments, was realized and measured. The coupling of the transformer windings avoids galvanic contacts between the antenna and the circuitry, allowing their isolation and passivation. The system operates in the ISM/SRD frequency band (2.4â2.4835 GHz), useful for Wi-Fi communication links in BAN. The ground plane reduces the interaction of the radiation with the human body. The simulations and the measurements show a good agreement, and the system provides a measured reflection coefficient below â10 dB in the overall operating frequency band. The results validate the use of this feeding topology, which can be implemented not only for patch antennas but also for other types of antenna. Moreover, the performed analysis about the effects of the windings misalignment on the system performance highlights its robustness.</p></div>
<h3>ACKNOWLEDGMENT</h3><p>This work was carried out during the Short Term Scientific Mission (STSM) supported by COST RFCSET.</p></div></div>

<script>

function getCookie(name) {
	var nameEQ = name + "=";
	var ca = document.cookie.split(';');
	for(var i=0;i &lt; ca.length;i++) {
		var c = ca[i];
		while (c.charAt(0)==' ') c = c.substring(1,c.length);
		if (c.indexOf(nameEQ) == 0) return c.substring(nameEQ.length,c.length);
	}
	return null;
}

	{
		if(IBP_WS_ENABLED_FLAG){
		Modal.refreshLegacyAccountTransition('/xpl/mwLegacyAccountTransition.jsp');
		}
	}
});

</script>

The coupling mechanism is based on a heterogeneous transformer with the primary and secondary windings implemented in the antenna and in the circuitry substrate (hybrid or monolithic), respectively. The proposed coupling topology avoids galvanic contacts between the antenna and the active circuitry, allowing for interconnecting the antenna by a mere placing and gluing process. Bonding and soldering processes, often critical for textile materials, are thus avoided. The proposed innovation, mitigating the industrial realization constraints posed by textile implementation of active antennas and radio frequency identification (RFID) tags, should unsurprisingly enable and foster industrial developments of garment RFID, wearable electronics and body-centric communications systems. A patch antenna with transformer based on textile materials and operating in the frequency band 2.4-2.4835 GHz is designed, realized, and measured.","keywords":[{"type":"IEEE
